He thought you would come. And since Casius M. \u003Cstrong\u003EClay\u003C\/strong\u003E has given the people his abolition views together \u0026amp; whither[?] his Whiggery it seems the reasons are more urgent that some one should deliver an [\u003Cem\u003Esic\u003C\/em\u003E] Liberty address soon in this Town, Cherry Creek and Arkwright. It is about 9 miles from Sinclairville to Arkwright, 6 or 7 to Villenova 7 or 8 to Cherry Creek and less distance to Ellington.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cI suppose you have been to the Convention at Utica[.] we should very much like to hear from it by a person that was there. He and his wife Abigail were founding members of the Freewill Baptist Church in Villenova the next year. The term \u201cLiberty Friends\u201d was thought by Judge E. T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E son to have been his father\u2019s code name for the Underground Railroad. \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E was one of the ten children of Salmon \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1786 \u2013 1835\u003C\/em\u003E) and Jane \u003Cstrong\u003ERippeth\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1790 \u2013 1866\u003C\/em\u003E). His birthday is sometimes listed as 25 August 1817, and sometimes as 25 April 1814, probably because the tombstone is difficult to decipher.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EOn June 6, 1839, \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E married Sarah (Sally) \u003Cstrong\u003EMoore\u003C\/strong\u003E in Eden NY, where they were living at the time of the 1840 Census. On 4 April 1844, he bought a 53-acre parcel of land in Cherry Creek, where he was listed in 1850 with his wife Sarah and one-year old son Franklin. Their farm may be seen on the 1854 county wall map near the town line between Cherry Creek and Villenova, but the home was most likely replaced by a larger home in the late nineteenth century.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBy April 1855, they had relocated to Dayton NY, where they continued to reside into 1860, and where Sarah probably died. In 1870, \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E and Esther were listed with a three-year old son, Nathan E. \u003Cstrong\u003EBeardsley\u003C\/strong\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe family was still listed in Villenova in 1880, and their Greek Revival home may be seen on the Villenova map in the 1867 county atlas. Isaac and Dilly \u003Cstrong\u003ECobb\u003C\/strong\u003E settled in Gerry in 1820 along with their sons Freeman, Isaac, John, and Rowland. Rowland \u003Cstrong\u003ECobb\u003C\/strong\u003E was involved with lumbering. In 1837 he built a store and house in Gerry and was in business there for the next two years. The 1840 Census recorded him living in Ellicott NY, the next town to the south.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the1845 list of church members, \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E did not list a church affiliation next to \u003Cstrong\u003ECobb\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E name. In fact, \u003Cstrong\u003ECobb\u003C\/strong\u003E was leaving the area by the time of the 1845 list. At a conference in Alexander NY on 28 August 1842, \u003Cstrong\u003ECobb\u003C\/strong\u003E was appointed to travel and assist in building up the Church. R.) \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E (1827 \u2013 1907) was a member of the anti-slavery movement in Chautauqua County before the Civil War. Many years later, the 3 May 1902 issue of the \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Evening Journal\u003C\/em\u003E carried an article by (C. R.) \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E, describing an interview he had with Mrs. Catharine \u003Cstrong\u003EHarris\u003C\/strong\u003E, an elderly African-American resident of Jamestown with a vivid memory of the Underground Railroad activities in which she had participated. \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E, a local attorney, was then 75. In the course of the article, he explained some of his motivation in writing the piece. He knew that Jamestown had been \u201ca way station on the line\u201d of the local Underground Railroad, but he said that he was \u201cnot cognizant of the inside workings.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETherefore, he said, \u201cWe solicited information from others, among whom we recently called upon our aged friend, Mrs. Catharine \u003Cstrong\u003EHarris\u003C\/strong\u003E. From her we have been pretty thoroughly informed; and the threads of progress are so interwoven in our local fabric that we feel in duty bound to spread it before the people.\u201d Later in the article \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E explained that he also questioned Phineas \u003Cstrong\u003ECrossman\u003C\/strong\u003E, who had been active in the local Underground Railroad. \u003Cstrong\u003ECrossman\u003C\/strong\u003E corroborated all that Mrs. \u003Cstrong\u003EHarris\u003C\/strong\u003E had said, adding some names to the list of agents, including Dr. James \u003Cstrong\u003ECatlin\u003C\/strong\u003E of Sugargrove PA.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECoincidentally, \u003Cstrong\u003ECatlin\u003C\/strong\u003E may well be a relative of the A.R. \u003Cstrong\u003ECatlin\u003C\/strong\u003E,who was an old friend of Lockwood\u2019s. R. \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E life up to that date:\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cClark R. \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in the town of Schroon, N.Y., June 6th, 1827, the fourth in a family of eight children. His father was Jeremiah \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E; his mother was Amanda \u003Cstrong\u003ERawson\u003C\/strong\u003E, daughter of Safford \u003Cstrong\u003ERawson\u003C\/strong\u003E, an old resident of Schroon. Clark R.\u2019s grandfather, Jeremiah \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E, with his family, moved from Berkshire county, Mass., in the year 1810, to Schroon, where he remained until he died. At the time of this removal Jeremiah jr. was a lad of about thirteen years, his birth having occurred May 17th, 1797. His mother was born in Schroon in the year 1800, and died at their home June 22nd, 1850, and his father died at the residence of Charles \u003Cstrong\u003EFowler\u003C\/strong\u003E, in Chestertown, Warren county, N.Y., April 19th, 1869. Both were buried in Schroon Cemetery, situated on the banks of Schroon river, but a short distance from their old home.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cFor many years Jeremiah jr. kept the \u201c\u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E tavern\u201d situated about two miles north of Schroon lake. There he raised his family of children, and they were educated principally in the common schools of the town. He also had a small farm on which he resided, and which he carried on by the aid of his children. Hotel keeping, at that time and place, was more of an accommodation to the public than of benefit to the owner, and but small profit was derived therefrom.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cClark R. remained at home until about sixteen years old, when he engaged with Jonathan \u003Cstrong\u003EStevens\u003C\/strong\u003E, of Castleton, Vt., as an apprentice at wagon-making, but remained only about two years, when, because of illness, he abandoned the enterprise and returned to his home. For several months he remained there and recruited, and then attended select school in Ticonderoga, N.Y., for about six months, and subsequently in Poultney, Vt., for several months. Then for a short time he engaged in teaching, after which he went to Chambly, Canada, to acquire a knowledge of the French language, and there obtained a situation in a French family, from whom he received instruction, but remained only about eight months, on account of sickness in his father\u2019s family. Being now about twenty-one years of age he again felt the necessity of choosing a business for life. He had no means except the little acquired from teaching. His health was poor, though improving, but, fully resolved upon making a \u201cstrike,\u201d he, through the assistance of Mr. A. R. \u003Cstrong\u003ECatlin\u003C\/strong\u003E, an old friend residing in Jamestown, obtained a situation as student in the law office of Mr. Orsell \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E, a leading lawyer in western New York.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cHe taught school during the winter of 1848-49 and with the wages thus earned he paid a few debts, bought a small amount of clothing, and with the remainder, less than $20, he left his home about August 23d, 1849, for the office of Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E in Jamestown, where he commenced reading \u003Cem\u003EBlackstone\u003C\/em\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cFor a time he resided in the family of Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E, doing chores to pay his board, but finding it necessary to replenish his funds, he engaged in teaching in the old \u201cPine street school-house\u201d for the term of six months, and during that time wrote for the office, boarded himself, and kept up his law reading. In the fall and winter of 1852-53 he attended the \u003Cstrong\u003EFowler\u003C\/strong\u003E Law School, at Ballston Spa, N.Y., and in the spring of 1853 was admitted to practice at a General Term of the Supreme Court, held at Buffalo. He was married, July 6th, 1853, to Miss Eunice E. \u003Cstrong\u003EWheeler\u003C\/strong\u003E, daughter of Nehemiah \u003Cstrong\u003EWheeler\u003C\/strong\u003E, then residing in Schroon. After his marriage he returned to the office of Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E, and, after a few months, formed a law partnership with Mr. W. M. \u003Cstrong\u003ENewton\u003C\/strong\u003E, with whom he continued practicing his profession under the name of \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E \u0026amp; \u003Cstrong\u003ENewton\u003C\/strong\u003E down to about August 25th, 1855, at which time he and Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E entered into partnership under the firm name of \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E \u0026amp; \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E. This last law firm has existed down to the present time, except six years, during which Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E was county judge of Chautauqua county.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cClark R. was many years since admitted to practice in the United States Courts, and has had considerable practice therein. Jamestown has been his residence ever since 1849. \u003Cstrong\u003ECook\u003C\/strong\u003E \u0026amp; \u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E have done a large law business, and are well and favorably known in western New York, where their practice has mostly been.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cClark R. has always been characterized for his great industry and perseverance, and in his professional practice he has met with merited success. In politics he was reared a Democrat, but as time advanced and party agitation developed the evils of slavery he felt it his duty to, and did, follow the leading thought of the country in protesting against slavery\u2019s assumed right of extension. He aided in the formation of the Republican party of his county, the fundamental principles of which he has ever cherished, and with the advocates of which he is yet identified. Although an active worker for the promotion of his principles he has not been an office seeker. In religious convictions he is a Universalist, liberal in thought and tolerant toward others. He has two children living, Olive Amanda, wife of Abner E. \u003Cstrong\u003EAllen\u003C\/strong\u003E, and Clark Wheeler [\u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E]. A daughter, Lizzie W., died several years since. He is yet active in his professional labors, and his promptness at the \u2018post of duty\u2019 evidences his will to do while there is work to be done.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ELockwood\u003C\/strong\u003E continued to live in Jamestown. However, he built a mansion on South Main Street (Warren Street) on the site of the former Rev. Chapin home. The 1900 Census gave his home address as 107 Warren Street. When the college\u2019s \u003Cem\u003EAnnual Report\u003C\/em\u003E (1919) noted the death of Samuel\u2019s daughter Emeline \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E of \u201cthe Class of 1852, a long time resident of Oberlin,\u201d the report said that her passing \u201ccalls attention to the fact that representatives of her family have been connected almost continuously with the College since its earliest years. Mrs. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E was the daughter of Deacon Samuel \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E, who with his brother Hezekiah \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E . . . came from Connecticut in 1819.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThat date would concur with the genealogical publication \u003Cem\u003ENew England Families \u003C\/em\u003Eby William R. \u003Cstrong\u003ECutter\u003C\/strong\u003E (Lewis Historical Publishing, Co., New York, 1913), which noted that Martin Luther \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E \u201ccame to Ohio with his parents in 1819.\u201d \u00a0A number of sources indicate that Martin Luther \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E later attended Oberlin \u201cand became a fearless opponent of slavery.\u201d The \u003Cem\u003EAnnals of the Early Settlers Association of Cuyahoga County\u003C\/em\u003E, Vol.4 (Cleveland, 1899), described Martin\u2019s influence on Oberlin in much the same way that histories of the college describe it. According to the story, while attending the college, Martin heard the famous abolitionist William Lloyd \u003Cstrong\u003EGarrison\u003C\/strong\u003E speak and then \u201cbecame imbued with the anti-slavery spirit.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhen Martin Luther \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E was designated the student speaker for a 4th of July 1833 celebration, he gave an ardent speech about abolition. It was \u201cthe first speech ever delivered in Oberlin on abolition, coming out fairly and squarely against slavery, thereby causing much excitement and talk in the little town\u2026. It is popularly supposed that Oberlin College was founded on anti-slavery principles from the first, but this is erroneous, as the trustees did not adopt the principles of anti-slavery till some time after young Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u2019\u003C\/strong\u003E speech.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003ECutter\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cem\u003EThe Genealogical and Family History of Western New York\u003C\/em\u003E (Lewis Historical Publishing Co., New York, 1912) noted that the patriarch Hezekiah \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E, \u201cwas [already] an ardent anti-slavery man and his home was a refuge for those of the colored race who attempted to escape from bondage to freedom. He and his family were members of the Congregational church.\u201d The abolitionist attitudes of Hezekiah \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E and his son Martin Luther \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E would help explain the anti-slavery activities of Dr. James \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E and Melvina \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E when James and Melvina moved to Ellington.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAfter graduating from Oberlin, James \u003Cstrong\u003EBrooks\u003C\/strong\u003E had entered the medical department of Western Reserve College, graduating in February 1846. T.\u003Cstrong\u003E Foote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E response to a correspondent, and in that response, \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E listed towns and villages in Chautauqua County. Next to each town or village, he entered the names of local people, sometimes noting a person\u2019s affiliation with a local church. Next to most names, \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E wrote a brief comment indicating the individual\u2019s attitude toward slavery and abolition.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor example, the Fredonia entry listed \u201cDr. J. \u003Cstrong\u003EPetit\u003C\/strong\u003E [\u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E] and H. \u003Cstrong\u003EClough\u003C\/strong\u003E\u201d with the comment \u201cActive liberty men.\u201d Also in the Fredonia entry was the following: \u201cRev[.] Mr[.] \u003Cstrong\u003EPomeroy\u003C\/strong\u003E[,] Presbyterian recently there from Illinois[,] profess[ed] abolit[ionist].\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAugustus Pomeroy was born on 2 October 1795 to Elihu and Lydia (\u003Cstrong\u003EBarber\u003C\/strong\u003E) \u003Cstrong\u003EPomeroy\u003C\/strong\u003E in Belchertown MA. He attended Williams College, then Andover Theological Seminary, where he graduated in 1824. Under the patronage of the United Domestic Missionary Society of New York, he was sent to Missouri in 1825 to encourage the establishment of Presbyterian Societies there. Under the same patronage and for the same purpose, he was sent in 1827 to Ohio. On 31 March 1829 he married Susan L. \u003Cstrong\u003ELincoln\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EMerwin\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECushing\u003C\/strong\u003E of Gallipolis OH.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESusan \u003Cstrong\u003ELincoln\u003C\/strong\u003E had been married first to John \u003Cstrong\u003EMerwin\u003C\/strong\u003E. After \u003Cstrong\u003EMerwin\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E death, she married Gen. Nathaniel S. \u003Cstrong\u003ECushing\u003C\/strong\u003E on 21 December 1819. She had been widowed again before marrying the Rev. \u003Cstrong\u003EPomeroy\u003C\/strong\u003E. In addition to his pastoral duties, Rev. \u003Cstrong\u003EPomeroy\u003C\/strong\u003E helped establish the National Home Missionary Society, and in 1832 the Female Seminary at Chillicothe OH. He continued his work in Ohio until February 1839, when he resigned due to ill health.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESusan died in 1844, and on 21 October 1845 Rev. \u003Cstrong\u003EPomeroy\u003C\/strong\u003E, then of Fredonia NY, married Margaret (\u003Cstrong\u003ECrawford\u003C\/strong\u003E) \u003Cstrong\u003EHawley\u003C\/strong\u003E of Saratoga NY, widow of Samuel \u003Cstrong\u003EHawley\u003C\/strong\u003E. Rev. Pomeroy was first mentioned in the Fredonia Presbyterian Church records as being present on 14 January 1845. A brief history of the church in the \u003Cem\u003EFredonia Censor\u003C\/em\u003E of 9 February 1933 gave his tenure as 1845 \u2013 1847. Nathaniel \u003Cstrong\u003EWest\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in Ulster, Ireland, in September 1794. Nothing is known of his parents. He studied theology in Edinburgh and was ordained in the Presbyterian Church in 1820. He continued in Edinburgh as a missionary for a few years and was one of the founders of the first temperance society there. On 24 September 1820 he married Sarah \u003Cstrong\u003ELinforth\u003C\/strong\u003E of Leeds in York, England. The family emigrated to the United States, arriving at New York on the Dalmarnock from Leith, Scotland, on 27 May 1834. The family listed on the ship\u2019s manifest included Nathaniel, 36, Sarah, 37, Jane Ann 12, Nathaniel \u003Cstrong\u003EWest\u003C\/strong\u003E, Jr., 8, and Sarah, 3.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERev. \u003Cstrong\u003EWest\u003C\/strong\u003E was installed as pastor of the Presbyterian church at Meadville PA on 11 May 1836. White - Cadiz Blacksmith\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMany of the inhabitants of Cadiz will remember the little white house and shop at the crossing of Main Street and the Ischua Creek in the Village of Cadiz where for twenty years lived and wrought William B. \u003Cstrong\u003EWhite\u003C\/strong\u003E, the blacksmith.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHe was born near great forests still inhabited by wild beasts and Indians, and were in constant fear of the British. The stories of the press-gangs, whose business it was to kidnap men and boys to take them to sea, so wrought upon the mother\u0027s mind, that she took her children (one daughter and three sons) and went back to her early home, near Northfield, Massachusetts. Her husband died soon after and she was left alone to support and train her children. How thoroughly this was done may be inferred from the fact that though forced to earn their breed by her work as a seamstress, she brought them up to fear God and love their country and their fellowman. She also gave them all a common school education and the three boys received trades. They all grew up to be Christians and each of the four families attested their patriotic training by sending a son to the army in the late Civil War.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAfter learning his trade, William went to Springfield, Massachusetts where he was married in 1833 to Nancy \u003Cstrong\u003ELeonard\u003C\/strong\u003E, who died some six years ago. In 1835 they moved to Cadiz, where he worked at his trade until his health failed and the family moved to Hinsdale. In 1869, they went to St. Charles, Minnesota.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELong years of bodily infirmity shut him out in a great measure from active life and its duties. Looking back on this, we see a man of vigor and energy, with a quick sense of humor of dedicated convictions, conscientious and faithful to duty. He was a zealous Baptist, an active temperance man and an abolitionist, when the name was a term of reproach. He kept up the village prayer meetings for years and was always in his place. In his father\u0027s family there was some mechanical and musical genius and he shared in these sufficiently to be interested in both subjects.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDuring his last years he never wearied in talking of the old days when horse nails and shoes, hoes, forks, chains, etc. were forged by hand from a \u0022Russian bar\u0022 and contrasting them with these days of invention and mechanical improvement. His musical genius found expression through his son, George L. \u003Cstrong\u003EWhite\u003C\/strong\u003E, now of Fredonia, New York who trained and conducted the world famed Fisk University Jubilee Singers, in their great work of founding that institution of learning for Freedmen (former slaves).\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHis old neighbors and friends in Cadiz remember how weakness and pain crippled his body and wrought lines of suffering and despondency in his countenance. He entered into rest at the home of his daughters in St. Charles, Minnesota on February 1, 1888 in the eightieth year of his age.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-78.932604783034,42.464993998234]},"properties":{"name":"Harris","description":"\u003Cp\u003EThe Rev. Thompson Steele \u003Cstrong\u003EHarris\u003C\/strong\u003E (1799 \u2013 1842) was born on 8 May 1799 in New Jersey. He attended the College of New Jersey in 1816 and the Princeton Theological Seminary in 1818. When he graduated in 1821, he was appointed missionary for the Buffalo Creek Reservation. According to \u003Cstrong\u003ESpafford\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cem\u003EGazetteer\u003C\/em\u003E, the reservation ran easterly about 18 miles along Buffalo Creek from Lake Erie. Samuel Gregory \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in Litchfield CT on 6 June 1797 to Miles and Lydia (\u003Cstrong\u003EGibbs\u003C\/strong\u003E) \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E. After his father\u2019s premature death in 1813, he worked as a farm hand. He entered Yale in 1819 but then transferred to Hamilton College, graduating in 1822. He was endorsed to preach in 1824, the year he married Clarissa \u003Cstrong\u003EGregory\u003C\/strong\u003E at Deposit NY. Between 1826 and 1837 he served a variety of churches in Central and Western New York, finally settling in Ripley NY where he served as Pastor of the Presbyterian Church until 1853. His first sermon in Chautauqua County was delivered in April 1831 in Ripley, where his brother-in-law the Rev. D. D. \u003Cstrong\u003EGregory\u003C\/strong\u003E was acting as temporary supply for a few months.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERev. \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E was present at the Anti-Slavery meeting of 29 August 1839 in Fredonia, and the minutes were published in the \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Weekly Journal\u003C\/em\u003E on 25 September 1839.\u00a0 According to that report, \u201cthe Rev. Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E of Ripley. . . . moved the second resolution, which he supported by some forcible remarks, which was adopted. \u003Cem\u003EResolved\u003C\/em\u003E, that the influence of slavery in preventing the access of the gospel to the slave is sufficient in itself to array the church of God against its continuance in any part of our common country.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ERev.\u003Cstrong\u003E Orton\u003C\/strong\u003E corresponded with E. T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E. In the Anti-Slavery collection of \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.mcclurgmuseum.org\/collection\/archives\/elial_t_foote_papers\/elial_t_foote_papers.html\u0022\u003Ethe Foote papers\u003C\/a\u003E\u00a0 is a long letter, dated 5 August 1844, from \u201c\u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E at Ripley\u201d replying to a letter of \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E regarding what \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E annotated as \u201ccounty anniversaries.\u201d It was the custom of the 1830s and 1840s to hold an annual meeting composed of \u201cbenevolent\u201d groups such as bible societies, temperance societies and antislavery societies. \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E wrote, \u201cAs you say, the Antislavery cause was in former years connected with our anniversaries but it [antislavery] had not then assumed as it has since a political attitude. It was at the time discussed in its moral bearings.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cAnd we desire to keep our county anniversaries as far removed as possible from giving any pretext to the charge of political maneuvering. At least the committee would consider themselves as hardly authorized to bring forward the abolition cause in its present attitude on the ground of their own responsibility without having the principle first settled by the voice of a county convention.\u201d He then went on to suggest a separate conference, after the anniversary meeting had concluded. \u201cI do not know that all antislavery men will see alike in reference to all measures that may be proposed but to hear, inquire \u0026amp; discuss can do none of us any harm,\u201d \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E wrote.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThis explains a comment about \u003Cstrong\u003EOrton\u003C\/strong\u003E that \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E made a year later. In 1793 the family removed to Seneca County NY, where William, Jr. studied law and was admitted to the bar. He was the first lawyer to open an office in Penn Yan, where he practiced for 12 years. Although a Quaker, he enlisted in the War of 1812, becoming captain of an artillery company. According to historical accounts in Penn Yan, when he was 37, \u003Cstrong\u003EShattuck\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E religious principles led him to give up the practice of law, a military career, and membership in the Masons. From Penn Yan he removed briefly to Pennsylvania, and then in 1842 to Randolph NY, where he remained.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EPenn Yan historical accounts further noted that \u201che was a contributor to various temperance and abolitionist newspapers, and in fact published in 1843 a small book entitled, \u003Cem\u003EAntidote for infidelity, superstition, sectarian bigotry, violence and oppression. \u003C\/em\u003EHe remained very active in the reform movements of the time.\u201d He was married at least twice, and his last wife was Maria \u003Cstrong\u003EHart\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EShattuck\u003C\/strong\u003E (1804 \u2013 1897). The census records indicate that he was a Baptist preacher who moved around quite a bit. In 1863, for example, he was listed in the Stockton, NY delegation to the annual meetings of the Erie Baptist Association.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHowever, the 1844 letter was dated Versailles in northwestern Cattaraugus county, and read as follows: \u201cHon. \u0026amp; Dear Sir, On the recommendation of Friends I take the liberty to address you. Our sheet must be necessarily small at first \u2014 but shall enlarge it as soon as consistent. We are ready to publish \u0026amp; are only waiting for the returns from the Prospectus. We should like to hear from you by the 20th or, at least, the 25th inst.\u00a0 If you send us the names first \u2013 it will answer if the money of company subscribers is sent on receiving the first no [number]. Single subscribers (?) in making their first payments. We hope to make the Paper such as the Friends of the cause will delight to sustain. The Annual meeting of the Erie co. Soc\u00a0 is to be held at Lodi \u2013 [now Gowanda, NY] on the 15th \u0026amp; 16th of Jany next. Can you not make it your convenience to be present? Yours truly in behalf of the slave, \u00a0L. Joseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in Oneida County NY in 1791. He was the son of Joseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E and Mary \u003Cstrong\u003EShepard\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1763 \u2013 1845\u003C\/em\u003E). With his brothers Ralph (\u003Cem\u003E1795 \u2013 1865\u003C\/em\u003E) and Alvin (\u003Cem\u003E1802 \u2013 1877\u003C\/em\u003E), Joseph came to Buffalo, NY before the War of 1812, then to Fredonia, NY in 1816. Joseph and Ralph had a store in Fredonia where the \u003Cstrong\u003EWoleben\u003C\/strong\u003E Block now stands. Joseph built the Fredonia home later occupied by John \u003Cstrong\u003ECrane\u003C\/strong\u003E where the Methodist Church now stands. In 1819 Ralph and Joseph sold the store and their ashery to Walter \u003Cstrong\u003ESmith\u003C\/strong\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJoseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E first wife was Deborah \u003Cstrong\u003EWilbur\u003C\/strong\u003E. She was born in 1794 and married Joseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E in October 1815 at New Hartford, Ontario County, NY. She died on 26 October 1820 in Fredonia. She is buried in Pioneer Cemetery.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJoseph relocated to Lodi (today\u2019s Gowanda) where he involved himself in many causes. He was President of the New York State Society for the Promotion of Temperance in 1830, Treasurer of the American Tract Society in 1837, an agent for the \u003Cem\u003EOberlin Evangelist\u003C\/em\u003E in 1841, and one of the founders of the Anti-Slavery Society of Western New York in 1842. In 1850 he was listed in Collins with wife Caroline and sons Albert, Charles, and Joseph still at home. He was nominated by the Liberty Party for the office of Lieutenant Governor.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ECaroline died in 1851 and was buried in Pine Hill Cemetery.\u00a0 Joseph\u2019s mother Mary, who had died in 1845, was also buried there. L. \u003Cstrong\u003EChaplin\u003C\/strong\u003E, candidate for Governor of New York on the Liberty Party ticket, was just then visiting him and that he had not yet heard from \u201cDr. \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E.\u201d A letter of 30 December 1844 refers to his involvement with the Erie County Anti-Slavery Society meeting about to occur.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJoseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E died on 25 May 1870 and was buried in Gowanda\u2019s Pine Hill Cemetery, as was his brother Ralph. Alvin \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E was an anti-slavery activist in Westfield and other parts of Chautauqua County, NY, and was buried in Westfield.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJoseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E obituary appeared in \u003Cem\u003EThe Fredonia Censor \u003C\/em\u003Eof 1 June 1870, as follows: \u201cDeath of Hon. Joseph \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E. \u2014 The Buffalo [newspaper] contains a notice of the death of this [pioneer] of Chautauqua county. He died at Cattaraugus, on the 25th inst. at the age of 79 years. Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E came to Buffalo before the War of 1812, where he remained in business for a short (?) time, and then came to Fredonia, where he [engaged in] mercantile business for several years. He was one of the founders of the Anti-Slavery Society of Western New York, and has lived to see the triumph of its principles, and its incorporation into the Constitution of the United States. Mr. P. through his long life was [a model of] high character as a man of integrity and [Christian] example. His children all survive him. One daughter is the wife of Rev. J. G. \u003Cstrong\u003ECochrane\u003C\/strong\u003E, missionary to Persia, two sons are [engaged in the] Christian ministry, and another Mr. [\u003Cem\u003EEdward L.\u003C\/em\u003E] \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E, for some time represented our government as Charg\u00e9s d\u0027affaires \u00a0to Mexico, and more recently has been Consul General at Havana.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA reprint of his obituary in \u003Cem\u003EThe Fredonia Censor \u003C\/em\u003Eof 8 June 1870 added a few details, as follows: \u201cTHE LATE JOSEPH PLUMB. \u00a0A correspondent from Portland says: You might add to the notice published last week, the fact that Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E first wife died while he was a resident of Fredonia, years ago, and her remains rest in the old Cemetery there. M. \u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E, of New York, has for many years been a trusted Special agent to the Treasury Department, and has hosts of warm friends in this and Cattaraugus counties. This provision having been violated, an ejectment suit was brought, and maintained through five years of litigation. It was decided by the Court of Appeals, in December last, fully sustaining the validity of the condition. This decision (\u003Cstrong\u003EPlumb\u003C\/strong\u003E [\u003Cem\u003Evs.\u003C\/em\u003E] \u003Cstrong\u003ETubbs\u003C\/strong\u003E) will be of value to any land holder desiring to perpetuate the blessings of temperance upon soil contiguous to cities or elsewhere. We met on Saturday evening to nominate [?] and found (by the number present in this part men acted) that, as I suggested to you (when on our way to Ellington) that your visit here had left a favorable impression on the mind. The three parties in making the nominations went at least through town supervisor to [?] with the exception of superintendent on the Liberty ticket (viz. Esq. [?]) who we supposed was decidedly with us but found he was not. He was supported by the Whigs for the same office and consequently was elected. The Whig Collector was elected otherwise the Democratic ticket succeeded throughout.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cBut to see the spirit of opposition to Liberty and [?] principles or rather to those who aim to carry them out would be discouraging to men who were not acting under a sense of duty to their God and Country. When we take this view of the subject and realize that our feeble endeavors seem to be [?] and [?] and [?] we are enabled to venture forward trusting in the righteousness of our cause and feeling that the race is not to the swift nor the battles to the strong, but oh my Brother how much wisdom and grace we need to make us to be as wise as serpents and harmless as doves. We were disappointed in the result of the election. The highest calculation made of any by our [?] was forty-five votes. It ranged from 43 to 58.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cWe are perfectly satisfied with the result of the election as respects our own success, but to see the course that most pursue to sustain the scale of [?] or contrary to some: Go see a party neglect men of sound moral principle and elect to office those entirely destitute and to see the scenes of drunkenness and profanity that occur is sickening in the extreme, and I am often led to wonder and say within myself when will this place be delivered from those things that are so directly calculated to destroy our peace, happiness, and welfare, but feel that we ought ever to trust in Him who is able to deliver. I should be pleased to hear from you occasionally and how the good cause progresses. You will please excuse my liberty, my want of a knowledge of Language and definition of words. I am, dear Sir, your friend and well wisher in the good cause. J. Andrew and Eliza and their family remained in Ripley for twelve years. During the antebellum portion of that time, Andrew may have involved himself with the UGRR.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn 1868 they relocated to Red Wing MN, where their son David was living. They were living there when \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E came back to attend the 1873 Old Settlers Reunion in Fredonia and arranged to use Elial T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E historical collection to write \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County\u003C\/em\u003E (1875).\u00a0 The \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Evening Journal \u003C\/em\u003Eof 11 June 1873 noted, \u201cJudge E.T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E has arrived, and brought with him his collection of historical items, consisting of scrap books, etc. amounting to thirty-two volumes, and expects for weeks to assist Hon. Less than four years later, on 17 February 1877, Andrew \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E died. Fortunately, his history of Chautauqua County had been completed and published. \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E was buried in his home town of Warsaw NY.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.235243790603,42.096745671841]},"properties":{"name":"Foote","description":"\u003Cp\u003EElial \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E (E.T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E) (1796 \u2013 1877) was born in Gill MA to Samuel Foote (1770 \u2013 1848) and Eliza (\u003Cstrong\u003EDoolittle\u003C\/strong\u003E) \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E. In 1798 the family relocated to Sherburne NY, where Elial grew up. After his early education Elial studied medicine and was licensed by the Chenango County medical society, later receiving his MD degree. In 1815 he moved to Jamestown NY and established his medical practice, although after a few years ill health caused him to abandon it. Elial was elected to the State Legislature in 1819 and again in 1826 and 1827.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe judge and his papers.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHe was an Associate Judge from 1818 to 1823, and then he became a \u201cfirst judge\u201d of Chautauqua County, a position he held for 20 years. The \u201cFirst Judges\u201d of the Common Pleas were appointed by the governor and the state senate until 1846. In 1811, Zattu \u003Cstrong\u003ECushing\u003C\/strong\u003E was appointed the first \u201cFirst Judge\u201d of Chautauqua County, then Elial T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E in 1824, and then Thomas A. \u003Cstrong\u003EOsborne\u003C\/strong\u003E in 1843. Elial T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E was especially active in anti-slavery efforts in Chautauqua County in 1843 and supported the Liberty ticket in 1844. He removed to new Haven CT in 1845, and there resumed his medical practice for some 20 years.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EElial T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E\u2019s antislavery efforts are well documented in papers that he collected himself. He had begun gathering historical material as early as the 1820s, amassing a large collection of newspapers, reminiscences, letters, and memoranda. It all went with him when he moved to Connecticut, where he planned to write a history of Chautauqua County. Among those papers, now preserved by the Chautauqua County Historical Society in Westfield NY, there is a segment devoted to \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E anti-slavery involvement. Those papers may be found in Vol. 9 at the following: \u00a0\u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.mcclurgmuseum.org\/collection\/archives\/elial_t_foote_papers\/elial_t_foote_papers.html\u0022\u003Ethe Foote Papers\u003C\/a\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EElial\u2019s brother Samuel \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E, Jr. (\u003Cem\u003E1798 \u2013 1856\u003C\/em\u003E), was also involved in the abolition movement, and was cited as an attendee at the county anti-slavery meeting of 25 September 1839 in Fredonia. The minutes of that meeting are on file at the D. R. \u003Cstrong\u003EBarker\u003C\/strong\u003E Museum in Fredonia.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYet, Elial never did write the history of Chautauqua County. Instead, Elial\u2019s collection of historical papers was passed along to his anti-slavery colleague Andrew W. \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E, who did write a definitive history of the county. The papers had been passed from \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E to \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E in June 1873, during an Old Settlers Reunion, which was held at Fredonia, NY. The \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Evening Journal \u003C\/em\u003Eof 11 June 1873 noted, \u201cJudge E.T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E has arrived, and brought with him his collection of historical items, consisting of scrap books, etc. amounting to thirty-two volumes, and expects for weeks to assist Hon. A.W. \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E in the preparation of his history of Chautauqua County.\u201d\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn another \u003Cem\u003EJournal\u003C\/em\u003E issue \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E was quoted as referring to his \u201ctwenty-four large\u201d volumes, and the \u003Cem\u003ECensor\u003C\/em\u003E of 18 June 1873 noted, \u201cHere he pointed to 26 large folio volumes of historic scrap books on the stage as an evidence of his labor.\u201d Whatever the precise number of volumes, the collection was enormous, and it was given to \u003Cstrong\u003EYoung\u003C\/strong\u003E for his use.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ENames mentioned in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWestern New York and Pennsylvania residents mentioned in the anti-slavery section of the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers are added to this database and map wherever possible. Other persons mentioned in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers were correspondents with the Judge and\/or passed through the area on occasion. Examples follow:\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe name of William L. \u003Cstrong\u003EChaplin\u003C\/strong\u003E\u00a0 (1796 \u2013 1871) appears in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E papers. \u00a0\u003Cstrong\u003EChaplin\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in Groton MA in 1796. He studied law and began his practice in Groton. In 1836 he was invited by the American Anti-Slavery Society to lecture under their auspices. In 1837 he became General Agent of the Society for the State of New York. In 1850 he was nominated by the Liberty party as candidate for Governor of New York. He was active in the Underground Railroad. At one point he was arrested for assisting some former slaves to escape from Washington DC and was jailed for a time. His brutal treatment while in jail seems to have affected his mind at least for a time after his release.General John \u003Cstrong\u003ECochrane\u003C\/strong\u003E is another name mentioned in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers. \u003Cstrong\u003ECochrane\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in Palatine NY on 27 August 1813. His mother was Cornelia \u003Cstrong\u003ESmith\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECochrane\u003C\/strong\u003E, sister to Gerritt \u003Cstrong\u003ESmith\u003C\/strong\u003E. It was \u003Cstrong\u003ECochrane\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E home in New York City at 7 East 62nd Street that Gerritt \u00a0\u003Cstrong\u003ESmith\u003C\/strong\u003E was visiting when he died on 28 December 1874.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the anti-slavery section of the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers, there is a newspaper article mentioning Jerome Benjamin \u003Cstrong\u003EGarland\u003C\/strong\u003E. It was from the Cleveland \u003Cem\u003EPlain Dealer\u003C\/em\u003E \u201cExtra\u201d of 1 November 1844. The article featured the reprint of a letter, purporting to be from \u201cJas. G. \u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u003C\/strong\u003E,\u201d one of the founders of the Liberty Party. The lead-in to the letter reads: \u201cThe Steamboat \u003Cem\u003EConstitution\u003C\/em\u003E has just arrived at our Dock, from Buffalo. Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003E\u003Cem\u003EBirney\u003C\/em\u003E\u003C\/strong\u003E was on board and pronounced the following letter \u2018A THOROUGH FORGERY!\u2019\u201d The letter is dated 26 September 1844 and is addressed to \u003Cstrong\u003EGarland\u003C\/strong\u003E in Saginaw City, MI. The gist of the letter is that \u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u003C\/strong\u003E is ready to \u201cgo for Democratic men and measures\u201d and \u201cforego the agitation of the Slavery question in our State Legislature.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EJerome Benjamin \u003Cstrong\u003EGarland\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in New Hampshire in 1811 and had settled in Saginaw with his family by 1836. On 28 September 1844 he was a delegate at the Democratic county convention in Saginaw. Along with others, he urged the convention to nominate \u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u003C\/strong\u003E for State Representative. Although it was understood that \u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u003C\/strong\u003E was not a Democrat, \u003Cstrong\u003EGarland\u003C\/strong\u003E argued that he had in his possession a letter from \u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u003C\/strong\u003E stating that he was and had always been a Democrat. \u00a0It appears that \u003Cstrong\u003EGarland\u003C\/strong\u003E honestly thought the letter was legitimate. \u00a0\u003Cstrong\u003EBirney\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E categorical denial of authorship seems to have put that issue to rest.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EDr. James C. \u003Cstrong\u003EJackson\u003C\/strong\u003E of Clarksville NY was an active anti-slavery man in the Albany NY area. He published the \u003Cem\u003EAlbany Patriot\u003C\/em\u003E from 1840 to 1844. One of his letters to \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote \u003C\/strong\u003Eis a form letter dated 11-19-1844 requesting subscriptions to the paper. It was accompanied by a personal handwritten letter inviting \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E to a meeting in Albany and sending regards to Mrs. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E and the family. A handwritten letter of 6 August 1845 lists the lectures he will be giving in Chautauqua County and his expectation to see \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E while there. Another letter in the Anti-Slavery Collection, this one from Alvan \u003Cstrong\u003EStewart\u003C\/strong\u003E in Utica dated 16 May 1844 mentions seeing \u201c\u003Cstrong\u003EJackson\u003C\/strong\u003E, the Tract Agent.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAlvan \u003Cstrong\u003EStewart\u003C\/strong\u003E wrote to \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E on 16 May 1844. \u003Cstrong\u003EStewart\u003C\/strong\u003E was born in South Granville NY in 1790, worked as a teacher and then as a lawyer. He was practicing law in Utica during the formation the New York Anti-slavery Society and became its first president. His letter to \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E reads in part, \u201cHon. E. T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E, \u2026 I saw Mr. James C. \u003Cstrong\u003EJackson\u003C\/strong\u003E the permanent Tract Agent, who has this day packed up $1000 (?) worth of Tracts to go by the Pomeroy express expense $0.25 directed to the care of Geo. A. \u003Cstrong\u003EFrench\u003C\/strong\u003E Buffalo Forwarding Merchant, in a large sealed bundle for you [w]here you can get them. I do most sincerely rejoice that your eyes are opened to the overwhelming ruin which slavery is bringing down upon this Country. I trust that you will be able to make Chautauque understand, feel, honor and respect your just \u0026amp; self evident opinions, on this great question, \u2026. I hope you will not find it inconsistent with your sense of duty to take the field in behalf of the poor crushed southern man as well for the rights of all men, which are assaulted thro the Slave (?). I hope you will make your county take a Front Rank with the Cause of human Freedom. I shall be happy to hear from you in coming days \u0026amp; years \u0026amp; believe me, your friend, Alvan \u003Cstrong\u003EStewart\u003C\/strong\u003E.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMentioned in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers is Lewis \u003Cstrong\u003ETappan\u003C\/strong\u003E (1788 \u2013 1873). Lewis \u003Cstrong\u003ETappan\u003C\/strong\u003E corresponded with \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E in regard to \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E subscribing to anti-slavery publications that \u003Cstrong\u003ETappan\u003C\/strong\u003E was promoting. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E saved those letters as well as a copy of the American and Foreign Anti-Slavery Society Constitution in which the \u003Cstrong\u003ETappans\u003C\/strong\u003E were officers.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESometimes another person\u2019s name in the \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E Papers reveals a great deal about \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E himself. An example is Daniel \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u003C\/strong\u003E. He was born on 28 May 1791 in Bristol RI and graduated from Brown University. He was admitted to the bar and began his practice in Rome NY in 1814. He was elected to the New York State Assembly in 1826, 1827, and 1828. In 1826 and 1827, a colleague of \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E in the Assembly was E. T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E. \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u003C\/strong\u003E then was elected as a Jacksonian to serve in Congress, which he did from March 1831 through March 1837. Late in January of 1837, \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E, by then a First Judge of Chautauqua County, wrote to \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u003C\/strong\u003E in regarding the issue of slavery in the United States and the touchy issue of abolishing slavery in the District of Columbia. \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E lengthy response made one thing clear. \u201cI abhor slavery in its mildest form. I would make almost any sacrifice to get rid of it.\u201d Followed, of course, by \u201cBut. . . .\u201d \u003Cstrong\u003EWardwell\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E position was twofold. \u201cAgitating the subject in Congress will do nothing but aggravate the Evil.\u201d Besides which, since slavery was a sin, he said, it should be fought against in the churches, not in the public arena. No doubt this response was not what \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E had hoped to hear. However, it is the earliest dated document indicating \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E own involvement with anti-slavery, which continued in earnest in the 1840s after he had retired from the bench. \u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.237684600807,42.091200630768]},"properties":{"name":"Grandin","description":"\u003Cp\u003EDaniel Hunt \u003Cstrong\u003EGrandin\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1811 \u2013 1893\u003C\/em\u003E) and Louisa Waite \u003Cstrong\u003EGrandin \u003C\/strong\u003E(\u003Cem\u003E1813 \u2013 1890\u003C\/em\u003E)\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EBy Douglas H. S. Bailey, p.73. \u201cIt is reported that there was an Underground Railroad station also at Falconer. Bert Mosher relates that when in 1860 his father moved into the old Edward Work house, which formerly stood on the corner now [1934] occupied by the Falconer Bank, there was a room on the second floor, the only access to which was a very skillfully concealed entrance from above. The general supposition has been that this concealed room was used for secreting slaves who came to the place as an Underground Railroad station. The existence of this station, however, cannot be definitely established.\u201d However, the anti-slavery activist Dr. Elial Foote wrote a biographical account of Edward Work, whom he had known for 40 years as a friend and a medical patient. Work was born in Montgomery Twp. PA on 3 December 1773. He read law and then moved to Meadville around 1798. On 1 August 1807 with his friend Dr. Thomas R. Kennedy he bought some 1200 acres around the outlet of Chautauqua Lake. In the fall of 1807 and spring of 1808 he \u201cerected his hewed log block house on the north side of the outlet, a little northeast of the mill, where he resided until he erected the frame house where he died. About the year 1836, he sold his mills and real estate at Worksburg, except his family residence, and a few acres of contiguous land, and retired from the bustle of active life, having a competence for himself and family. His declining years were spent in retirement, mingling but little with society.\u201d\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-78.9871,42.3398]},"properties":{"name":"Wells","description":"\u003Cp\u003EJames Wells was born in VT around 1803. He is first recorded in Leon, Cattaraugus County, as a Road District Overseer in 1833. He and his family remained in Leon at least through 1865. James Wells\u2019 involvement in Underground Railroad activities was attested to by a stepson of Joseph B. Nessel (Nessle). \u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EW.S. Bailey in his article entitled \u201cThe Underground Railroad in Southwestern New York\u201d (\u003Cem\u003EThe Historic Annals of Southwestern New York\u003C\/em\u003E , William Doty, Ed., Lewis Historical Pub. Co., New York, 1940) noted, \u201cThe Ellington station was conducted by Joseph B. Nessle, Mr. Stafford\u2019s stepfather, at his home in Ellington village. Mr. Stafford well remembered the frequent signal at the door during the night and of hearing his stepfather open the door and admit the conductor and his party. With the curiosity of a boy, young Stafford often stole down to see the strange visitors whom he recalls as extremely shy and in constant dread of capture. After the slaves had been fed by Mr. Stafford\u2019s mother [Polly Stafford Nessel], Mr. Nessle would immediately harness his horses and the same night drive on to the next station which was conducted by James Wells two miles north of Leon Center. The Leon station was in a more secluded location and the slaves could be secreted there during the day with greater security. Mr. Stafford\u2019s only knowledge of the route beyond Leon was that it led to Buffalo.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere were three Stafford stepsons living in the Nessel family in the 1850s and 1860s, Austin, Joseph and Martin. He was born in Columbia, CT in 1825 or 1826. His wife, Mariah Whitney, was also born in Connecticut. They had settled in Chautauqua County in the Town of Charlotte at least by the time of the \u003Cem\u003E1850 Census\u003C\/em\u003E. Their household in 1850 included Luther Webster, 25 (listed as a farmer); Mariah Webster, 26; Huldah Chandler, 76, and Sophia Webster, 47, all born in Connecticut. Charles Webster was the only child of Luther Webster and Mariah Whitney. Luther Webster had apparently purchased his home from Daniel Pritchard. There were two men by this name in Fredonia in the nineteenth century, a father and son. The senior was known as Dr. Daniel Pritchard, a Methodist minister who lived in Chautauqua County from 1847 until his death in 1890. The son attended the Fredonia Academy and later settled back in this area as well.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ELuther Webster was listed in the \u003Cem\u003E1850 Census\u003C\/em\u003E as a farmer, in the \u003Cem\u003E1860 Census\u003C\/em\u003E as a retired farmer, in the \u003Cem\u003E1865 Census\u003C\/em\u003E as a farmer, in the \u003Cem\u003E1870 Census\u003C\/em\u003E as a retired farmer, in the \u003Cem\u003E1875 Census\u003C\/em\u003E without an occupation, and in the \u003Cem\u003E1880 Census\u003C\/em\u003E as \u201cat home.\u201d According to the census list of his neighbors, the Webster home in the Town of Charlotte is probably shown as an unnamed home on the 1854 county wall map. Benjamin Vincent was born in Rhode Island about 1805. He came to Villenova NY in 1834, setting up as a blacksmith, later just farming. His wife, Alvira, the daughter of Elisha Searle, was born about 1809 and came to Villenova with her family in 1832. Benjamin and Alvira were married by 1838. Pettit in \u003Cem\u003ESketches in the History of the Underground Railroad. \u003C\/em\u003EPettit\u00a0 four times refers to a mysterious \u201cFriend Andrew,\u201d an important Underground Railroad agent, apparently a Quaker. There are a few other clues in Pettit\u2019s account as to \u201cfriend Andrew\u2019s\u201d age and location. On p.14 of the 1999 annotated edition by Paul Leone, Friend Andrew has arrived at Black Rock by carriage, carrying \u201cDan,\u201d a runaway slave. By 1840 they were in Evans NY where they continued to live. Andrew Varney\u2019s house can be seen in the \u201cPontiac\u201d section in the 1866 atlas of Erie County in the Maps Division of the New York Public Library. Andrew Varney\u2019s involvement can be traced from his membership in the Troy-Pittstown meeting of 1819. On 1 September 1824 he became a member of the Starksborough monthly meeting and on 1 April 1825 he was married at the Hamburg monthly meeting to Mary Stevens.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ETheir son, Austin F. \u201cJames\u201d Varney was born in Brant NY in 1828. He moved with his parents to Cazenovia NY in 1808, attended the commons schools and was apprenticed to a harness maker and saddler. In December 1822 he began working for William Knight in Jamestown NY. He was in the military company that greeted LaFayette at Fredonia in 1824, and in 1825 Silas is said to have come to Fredonia (when he was 21), but in 1827 he returned to Jamestown and opened a harness and saddler shop at the corner of Main and Third streets. One of his later apprentices was John P. Shearman. On 29 March 1829 he married Mary C. Marsh, originally from Vermont. They lived their entire married life in Ellicott. An online post says he \u201cwas a democrat until the close of the Polk administration [1849], when he became an Abolitionist, and acted as a conductor on the underground Railroad, assisting slaves to reach Canada. After the Civil War, Mr. Shearman was a Republican, he was also a relentless foe of the liquor traffic and an advocate of equal suffrage.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAn online article about his home as an Underground Railroad station said, \u201cShearman was what is termed a rabid abolitionist at a time when active opposition to slavery was most unpopular. In the 1850s he became a conductor of the \u2018underground railroad\u2019 and his home was the principle station in Jamestown. It was not uncommon for him to come down in the morning and find his kitchen filled with escaping slaves who were brought to Jamestown during the night and directed to his home. He would feed them and hid[e] them during the day in his barn on Stillers Alley and then arrange for their transportation to the next station, often in the village of Ellington.\u201d \u201cHe was also active in the cause of temperance. As a result of his decided views upon both temperance and the slavery question, he lost many friends.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003ESilas Shearman died in Jamestown on 3 September 1891. A. Redington operated a foundry in Fredonia before the Civil War. By the time of the \u003Cem\u003E1851 Map of Fredonia\u003C\/em\u003E, the name of F. A. Redington was shown with a house on Mechanic Street. The E. A. Lester and F. A. Redington Furnace, also known as the Fredonia Foundry, was shown across the street on the creek. Frederic A. Redington and his friend Enoch A. Curtis, who would later build several homes in Fredonia as well as other parts of western NY and PA, lost their health while serving duty during the Civil War. In late 1864, Frederic A. Redington and his wife Dorinda C. Redington conveyed the Fredonia house to Enoch A. Curtis (\u003Cem\u003ELiber 116 Page 132\u003C\/em\u003E). The transaction was part of a complicated deal in which Redington deeded his house on Mechanic Street (today\u2019s Forest Place) and his hardware store in the Woleben Block on Main Street to Curtis for $4500. In turn, Redington received the Curtis farm and house lot in the township of Harmony for $3800.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn a letter to the editor of the Fredonia \u003Cem\u003ECensor\u003C\/em\u003E of 11 November 1885, Redington, then of Chicago, alluded to his affiliation with the anti-slavery movement, as follows: \u201cMy Dear Friend:\u2014I have received the copy of Dr. Pettit\u2019s history of the U.G.R.R. for which I thank you. It enables me to live over again the days when slavery began to find a voice here and there bold enough to condemn it and endure the scoffs of popular opinion which proclaimed slavery a divine institution from both platform and pulpit. I am reminded of an incident which occurred in Westfield fifty-five years ago when Rev. D. D. Gregory\u00a0 gave notice from the pulpit that on Monday evening there would be a meeting in the conference room of the church to organize an \u201canti-Slavery Society.\u201d At the time appointed a lad of sixteen winters and fifteen summers found the Rev. Gregory and another \u201ccontemptible abolitionist\u201d standing at the door which had been locked by order of the deacons who \u201cwould not consent to such desecration of God\u2019s House.\u201d The lad crawled into an unfastened window and admitted the crowd of two persons. The society was organized and officers chosen consisting of President, Secretary and Treasurer. The society grew and multiplied; the U.G.R.R. was constructed; political parties were rent asunder; the rail road trains ran noiselessly whenever there were one or more passengers. Canada was the Ark of safety to which the slave ran for dear life, and those who abetted their escape were criminals in the eyes of the law.What a lever that U.G.R.R. proved to be. It excelled the fancies of Archimedes. Three millions of broken yokes. Three millions of bondsmen set free. Forever sacred be the memory of Conductor Pettit and his co-workers. Yours Truly. F. A. Redington.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.233752482391,42.099643389868]},"properties":{"name":"Price","description":"\u003Cp\u003EWilson Andrews Price was born in Cortland NY on 24 February 1816 to Charles and Mary (Neff) Price. The \u003Cem\u003EBuffalo Gazette \u003C\/em\u003Eof November 1816 reported that J. \u0026amp; R. Plumb had a \u201cnew store at Canadaway Village,\u201d the item dated 21 October 1816. From 1820 Alvin clerked in Rochester and then Geneva NY, where he also attended the Geneva Academy. He resettled in Jamestown NY and by 1824 was in \u201cthe mercantile business\u201d as well as \u201cthe milling business 4 miles below Jamestown.\u201d In 1827 he formed a stock company which built and launched a side-wheel steamer, the Chautauqua, which made its first trip to Mayville on 4 July 1828. In 1832 he married Mary Ann Davis of Westfield. He was elected to the State Assembly in 1833 and later served as County Clerk for Chautauqua. After that he moved to Westfield where he bought and sold land. He was Town Supervisor there in 1848 and 1852. In fact, there are several building sites labeled as \u201cA. Plumb\u201d in various townships on the 1854 county wall map. Young\u2019s \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County\u003C\/em\u003E (p.609) said, \u201cHe has been an active friend of the temperance and anti-slavery causes.\u201d Plumb died on 13 May 1877. His Underground Railroad affiliation as a stationmaster was cited by William S. Bailey in \u201cThe Underground Railroad in Southwestern New York,\u201d \u003Cem\u003EThe Historic\u00a0Annals of Southwestern New York,\u003C\/em\u003E William Doty, Ed., Lewis Historical Pub. Co., New York, 1940.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.3531,42.4493]},"properties":{"name":"Pettit","description":"\u003Cp\u003EDr. James Pettit (1877 \u2013 1849) and his family moved to Fredonia from the Pompey area in the mid-1830s and settled on a small farm just northwest of the village in an area later nicknamed Cordova. It was there that Pettit operated his Underground Railroad station, which was cited by his son Eber M. Pettit in \u003Cem\u003ESketches in the History of the Underground Railroad\u003C\/em\u003E, Willard McKinstry, Fredonia, New York, 1879. Following the death of Dr. James Pettit,\u00a0 his daughter Harriet Pettit Handy (1810 \u2013 1878) and her husband Harley H. Handy (1813 \u2013 1896) remained at the family farm, as did Eber\u2019s mother Lucy Felt (Phelt) Pettit (1777 \u2013 1859). A short distance to the west, also in Cordova, was the family of Graphen Sweet (1774 \u2013 1841), who was also from the Pompey area and had arrived in Fredonia along with the Pettits. Sweet\u2019s daughter Euretta (1804 \u2013 1873) was the wife of Eber M. Pettit, and their daughter was Helen Pettit Barker (1825 \u2013 1880), wife of Darwin R. Barker (1820 \u2013 1885).\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EA Page from the County\u2019s Anti-slavery Society Journal, 1839.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn the late twentieth century, a typed document was donated by Esther Fay \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u003C\/strong\u003E to the D. R. \u003Cstrong\u003EBarker\u003C\/strong\u003E Museum in Fredonia, NY. The typewritten note at the top of the document said, \u201cThis being a page from the Journal of the Annual Meeting of the Chautauqua Anti-Slavery Society, held September 25, 1839.\u201d However, the rest of the document consists of minutes of the meeting, which were published\u00a0 almost verbatim in the \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Weekly Journal\u003C\/em\u003E of 25 September, 1839.\u00a0 The actual meeting was on August 29, 1839. Also, a note in the Museum states that the document came from (or is a copy of an item in) the scrapbook of \u201cMrs. Jesse \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E,\u201d who turns out to be Fay Irma \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E (Mrs. Jesse \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E).\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAlthough it would be helpful to know whether the typewritten item donated by Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1914 \u2013 2002\u003C\/em\u003E) came from a newspaper clipping in a scrapbook, or from an actual journal of the anti-slavery society, such information may never be known. Still, it is interesting to consider Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E family connections to the anti-slavery society of 1839. First of all, Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u003C\/strong\u003E had grown up at today\u2019s 245 Chestnut Street, the home of her parents, Fred Fay \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1883 - 1953\u003C\/em\u003E) and May \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1879 - 1964\u003C\/em\u003E). Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u003C\/strong\u003E later lived next door at today\u2019s 237 Chestnut Street, in the house formerly owned by her grandparents, Edwin \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1847 \u2013 1933\u003C\/em\u003E) and Clara Mathilda \u003Cstrong\u003EBunce\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1848 \u2013 1918\u003C\/em\u003E).\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EMs. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E aunt Fay Irma \u003Cstrong\u003EFrost\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1873 \u2013 1930\u003C\/em\u003E) inherited the house at 237 Chestnut Street. As she passed along the house to Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u003C\/strong\u003E, Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E also passed along the document now in the museum. How, though, did Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E acquire the document? The following reconstruction, although conjectural, seems too obvious to be ignored. Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E probably acquired the scrapbook item or journal through her husband, Jesse Melancthon \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1876 \u2013 1929\u003C\/em\u003E), who had inherited it from his parents, Frederick Worden \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1857 \u2013 1927\u003C\/em\u003E) and Lucy \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003ECase\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1854 \u2013 1877\u003C\/em\u003E). They had probably inherited the newspaper clipping or journal from Lucy\u2019s parents, Melancthon Smith \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1818 \u2013 1878\u003C\/em\u003E) and Sarah \u003Cstrong\u003EHancox\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E (\u003Cem\u003E1825 \u2013 1886\u003C\/em\u003E), who lived in the home which once stood at today\u2019s 104 Matteson Street.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThey, in turn, had probably inherited the clipping or journal from Melancthon\u2019s parents, Dr. James \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E (1\u003Cem\u003E767 \u2013 1849\u003C\/em\u003E) and Lucy \u003Cstrong\u003EFelt\u003C\/strong\u003E \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E (1768 \u2013 1859), who had operated an Underground Railroad station at the home and farm which once stood at today\u2019s 467 Chestnut Street. Dr. \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E was the president of the Chautauqua County Anti-Slavery Society in 1839, and was so named in the 1839 minutes. Additionally, the minutes named twenty-two of the more than seventy people present at the 1839 meeting. A. \u003Cstrong\u003EHazeltine\u003C\/strong\u003E, Rufus \u003Cstrong\u003EJones\u003C\/strong\u003E, Wm. \u003Cstrong\u003EBreed\u003C\/strong\u003E, G.
