After prayer, adjourned.\u003C\/em\u003E\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIt may next be wondered how upwards of seventy people could have heard about an anti-slavery meeting before the days of rapid communication. One answer to that question may be seen in newspaper notices that occasionally appeared about such meetings.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor example, a year earlier, the \u003Cem\u003EFredonia Censor\u003C\/em\u003E reported on 8 August 1838, \u201cThe Chautauque County Antislavery Society will hold their annual meeting at Westfield, on Wednesday the 29th inst. at 2 o\u2019clock, P.M. Abolitionists are respectfully invited to attend, and all who feel an interest in this glorious cause. The meeting will be held in the Presbyterian church. It is expected the meeting will be attended by several gentlemen from abroad. Rufus \u003Cstrong\u003EJones\u003C\/strong\u003E, Sec\u2019y., Jamestown, Aug. 8, 1838.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EYet, the anti-slavery meetings were not only announced in 1838, but were also held in conjunction with the meetings of other groups. For example, the \u003Cem\u003ECensor\u003C\/em\u003E of 8 August 1838 also reported, \u201cNotice is hereby given, that the Chautauque County Sunday School Union, Bible, Anti-Slavery, and Temperance Societies, will hold their respective Anniversaries at Westfield, in the Presbyterian Church, on the 29th and 30th of August next. There will be three public meetings during each day, commencing with the Sabbath School Anniversary on Wednesday at 9 o\u2019clock, A.M. Town directors of Sabbath Schools, are requested to prepare full statistical reports; and if possible, to present them in their own proper persons; or to forward them by some friend of the cause. Speakers have been secured within the county and from abroad to address the different Societies; and arrangements are made to entertain friends, and they are earnestly solicited to attend, and partake with us, of what is confidently expected to be a good intellectual and religious feast. By Ex. Com., David D. Gregory, Ch\u2019n. Westfield.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFinally, the anti-slavery meetings in 1838, like the meetings the following year, warranted even further attention from the press. The following appeared in the\u003Cem\u003E Censor \u003C\/em\u003Eof 5 September 1838:\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cTo the Editor of the Censor: Sir,\u2014Having been a gratified spectator of the proceedings at the anniversary meetings held at Westfield last week, I feel unwilling they should be suffered to pass off entirely without notice.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe first meeting was that of the Chautauque County Sunday School Union, Rev. Washington \u003Cstrong\u003EWinsor\u003C\/strong\u003E, President. The reports from the different towns exhibited an encouraging prospect of the success of these primary seminaries for forming good citizens and good society, and the speeches of several individuals present were well calculated to prompt to further exertion and more ardent zeal in so good a cause.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe Chautauque County Bible Society\u2014Hon. Elial T. \u003Cstrong\u003EFoote\u003C\/strong\u003E, President\u2014next held their meeting. The resolution adopted some two or three years ago by the society to furnish every family in the county with a Bible, it was discovered had involved the society in a debt of between seven and eight hundred dollars; and the great anxiety of the friends and supporters of the society was that this debt should be immediately cancelled; and the spirit manifested on the occasion, and the measures proposed by the preservering [\u003Cem\u003Esic\u003C\/em\u003E] president, and adopted, gave earnest that such would soon be the desirable result.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe Chautuauqe County Anti-Slavery Society\u2014R. P. \u003Cstrong\u003EJohnson\u003C\/strong\u003E, Esq. President\u2014next presented their claims to the community. Comparatively little has been done in this county in this cause of philanthropy and right; but the resolutions introduced and ably sustained by several individuals gave assurance that it was henceforth to take rank with the most benevolent institutions of this reforming age.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe Chautauque County Temperance Society\u2014Joseph \u003Cstrong\u003EWaite\u003C\/strong\u003E, Esq. President\u2014next claimed attention. This society it was apparent had done much good in lessening the evils of intemperance and abolishing the hitherto almost universal custom of using ardent spirits in the domestic and social circle; but it was apparent that much remained yet to be done to gain a complete victory over this monster vice. The absurdity of the license system was brought under especial notice, and a resolution adopted to use all laudable means to have amended or done away a law granting a \u003Cem\u003Ebounty\u003C\/em\u003E for the sale of ardent spirits\u2014a law that would disgrace even the dark ages.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe several objects of these different societies having been passed upon during the day and evening of Wednesday, on Thursday an interesting exhibition of the scholars and teachers of the Sabbath Schools of Westfield and those in its immediate vicinity took place; an exhibition well arranged and executed, with the single fault (and a fault altogether too common) of keeping the children, after marching, in a standing position for an hour or more to hear addresses.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe ceremonies were closed by a discourse in the evening upon the subject of anti-slavery, by the Rev. Mr. \u003Cstrong\u003EWest\u003C\/strong\u003E, of North East, Pa. a gentleman who had done much to give interest to the several topics discussed, having taken part in each of the discussions. To show that this subject is beginning to receive the attention its importance demands, we have only to state that the house was more completely filled upon this occasion than any other with an attentive audience. And this is one great point gained in this cause\u2014the days of mobism are in a measure over\u2014people are now willing to hear upon this as well as upon other subjects\u2014are willing to \u003Cem\u003Ediscuss\u003C\/em\u003E it, which is more than half in achieving a victory over oppression. The subject of the speaker was the sin of \u003Cem\u003Eneutrality\u003C\/em\u003E in this cause of benevolence which he illustrated and enforced by bible arguments, in a manner which I doubt not produced no very agreeable sensations among some of his reverend and moral professing brethren present; several of whom though acknowledging the great evil and sin of slavery, and \u003Cem\u003Eprofessing\u003C\/em\u003E a great desire for its being done away, are too deeply imbued with what one has aptly termed an \u201cirredeemable shinplaster morality\u201d to stem the popular opinion and act in accordance with their better feelings and sense of duty.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe good people of Westfield are deserving of much credit for their hospitality to strangers, and their exertions to give satisfaction to all. An Observer.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFinally, it is helpful to understand that national, state, and local anti-slavery societies were quite new. Even though abolitionists had been active at the informal level for decades, the American Anti-Slavery Society had not been formed until 1833, the New York State Anti-Slavery Society had only been formed in 1835, and local societies had only been formed in 1836, many with some difficulty.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor example, the \u003Cem\u003EFredonia Censor\u003C\/em\u003E reported in 1836, \u201cAnti-Slavery Society . --Certain of our good neighbors at Dunkirk had some trouble last week, in forming themselves in an anti-slavery society. A notice was given requesting those in \u201cfavor\u201d of such a measure to meet at a place appointed. It appearing, however, from various demonstrations, there would be a goodly number present who were not in favor of the measure, those friendly to the subject formed themselves into an anti-slavery society at another place, and then proceeded to ______ first designated place, where they in fact found a well filled room \u2013 officers appointed to their hands, and all things ready to proceed to business. After a great number of speeches were made and resolutions offered, (all those offered by the Abolitionists were voted down,) things were brought to a crisis, _____ a resolution offered and carried by the opposition, that one of the Abolitionists should be reprimanded by the chair, for offering an amendment that was deemed derogatory to the meeting. The ____ stepped out upon the floor to receive the reprimand, which placed the chairman in rather a quandary, and the reprimand was suffered to go by default. The meeting soon after broke up, both sides considering that they had carried their points. We understand the anti-Abolitionists are to have their proceedings published in the \u003Cem\u003EDunkirk Beacon\u003C\/em\u003E! , the \u003Cem\u003EAlbany Argus\u003C\/em\u003E!!, and \u003Cem\u003EWashington Globe\u003C\/em\u003E!!! \u2013 whether the other side are to be honored with any further notoriety than we here give them, we know not.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EHowever, in spite of the problems in Dunkirk in 1836, Ms. \u003Cstrong\u003EPelton\u2019s\u003C\/strong\u003E document as well as the newspaper accounts of the meetings in 1838 and 1839 tell us that a countywide anti-slavery society was operating without a great deal of protest by the end of that decade. In fact, by 1843, the \u003Cem\u003ECensor\u003C\/em\u003E reported, \u201cNotice \u2013 An Anti-slavery County Convention will be held at Sinclairville on Thursday the 19th inst. at 10 o\u2019clock A.M. to nominate suitable candidates for the Elective offices of the County at the ensuing election. Patriots \u2013 Friends of the equal rights of \u003Cem\u003Eall men\u003C\/em\u003E, rally in your strength from every town in the county to this gathering of Liberty-men. Many voters.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor further information about\u00a0James Pettit and his family, see \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.rootsweb.ancestry.com\/~nychauta\/CHURCH\/FredoniaBapt\/Miscellaneous\/Straight-EberMPettit.pdf\u0022\u003EEber Pettit and the Fredonia Baptist Church\u003C\/a\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Cem\u003ERandolph \u003C\/em\u003E(NY)\u003Cem\u003E Register\u003C\/em\u003E of 14 August 1903 published the following story, which was entitled, \u201cRelics of Slavery Days.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cLast week witnessed the destruction of a landmark of northern Chautauqua county, around which clustered many a story of ante-bellum and anti-slavery days. It was the old house at the corner of Matteson and Chestnut streets, at the very north limits of the village, once the home of Dr. James \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cDr. \u003Cstrong\u003EPettit\u003C\/strong\u003E was an anti-slavery man of the strongest convictions and in the days of the \u2018underground railroad\u2019 he was one of the \u2018conductors.\u201d\u2019The premises were then surrounded by a strong high board fence and as the stories go the poor fugitives fleeing for the protection of the Canadian border, were taken from the \u201cconductor\u201d next up the line, and kept until the next favorable opportunity, when the sturdy doctor, now dead these twenty years, would bundle them into his covered wagon and give them another lift toward Buffalo and the Niagara frontier.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cMany are the stories which are told of these trips, of the flying rides in the gloom and darkness of the hours just before dawn and the elusive tactics of the doctor to escape pursuit, by his knowledge of every turn and dark place in all the roads of this section, of detection and cross questioning from lawyers and justices intent upon breaking up the practice, and of the harrowing tales told by the escaping slaves.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u201cThe old place, finally, on the death of the doctor, passed out of the control of the family, and is now owned by Charley \u003Cstrong\u003EHonert\u003C\/strong\u003E, who is demolishing the building to make room for a modern dwelling.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-78.9924,42.5188]},"properties":{"name":"Pettit","description":"\u003Cp\u003EEber M. Pettit, also known as Dr. Eber M. Pettit, operated an Underground Railroad station in Versailles, NY. He cited his experiences there in \u003Cem\u003ESketches in the History of the Underground Railroad\u003C\/em\u003E, Willard McKinstry, Fredonia, New York, 1879. Pettit had arrived with his parents and in-laws in the Fredonia area in the mid-1830s. They had come from the Pompey area of central New York. Eber\u2019s father set up an Underground Railroad station in Fredonia, and by 1840, Eber Pettit and his wife shared a home and Underground Railroad station in Versailles with their daughter Helen, her husband Darwin R. Barker, and the Barker children. After the Emancipation Proclamation, Eber Pettit and the rest of the Versailles clan moved back to Fredonia and built a home on Central Avenue. Next door on Central Avenue, they continued to operate the Pettit Eye Salve medicine business which they had started in Versailles.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFor further information about Eber Pettit and his family, see \u003Ca href=\u0022http:\/\/www.rootsweb.ancestry.com\/~nychauta\/CHURCH\/FredoniaBapt\/Miscellaneous\/Straight-EberMPettit.pdf\u0022\u003EEber Pettit and the Fredonia Baptist Church\u003C\/a\u003E.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.0984,42.1592]},"properties":{"name":"Page","description":"\u003Cp\u003EVaranes Page, Jr. was probably also known as Surveyor Page. The Underground Railroad affiliation of Varanes Page as a conductor is cited by William S. Bailey in \u201cThe Underground Railroad in Southwestern New York,\u201d \u003Cem\u003EThe Historic \u00a0Annals of Southwestern New York\u003C\/em\u003E, William Doty, Ed., Lewis Historical Pub. Nessel (Nessle) was born in Saratoga NY in 1800. In 1830 he was living in Manlius NY but he had moved to Ellington by 1833. He and his brother were involved there in manufacturing from the 1830s. In 1853 he bought the Jamestown \u003Cem\u003EHerald\u003C\/em\u003E, moving it to Ellington as \u003Cem\u003EThe Ellington Herald\u003C\/em\u003E. As reported in \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County and its People\u003C\/em\u003E, John P. Downs and Fenwick Y. Bailey in his article entitled \u201cThe Underground Railroad in Southwestern New York\u201d (\u003Cem\u003EThe Historic Annals of Southwestern New York\u003C\/em\u003E, William Doty, Ed., Lewis Historical Pub. Co., New York, 1940) noted, \u201cThe Ellington station was conducted by Joseph B. Nessle, Mr. Stafford\u2019s stepfather, at his home in Ellington village. Mr. Stafford well remembered the frequent signal at the door during the night and of hearing his stepfather open the door and admit the conductor and his party. With the curiosity of a boy, young Stafford often stole down to see the strange visitors whom he recalls as extremely shy and in constant dread of capture. After the slaves had been fed by Mr. Stafford\u2019s mother [Polly Stafford Nessel], Mr. Nessle would immediately harness his horses and the same night drive on to the next station which was conducted by James Wells two miles north of Leon Center. The Leon station was in a more secluded location and the slaves could be secreted there during the day with greater security. Mr. Stafford\u2019s only knowledge of the route beyond Leon was that it led to Buffalo.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThere were three Stafford stepsons living in the Nessel family in the 1850s and 1860s, Austin, Joseph and Martin. They were active in the early Baptist congregation of Cassadaga. The family is well documented in the Stockton and Pomfret area as having been supportive of progressive causes which included the anti-slavery movement. \u00a0After the Civil War, the northerly portion of the Lyon farm was sold to the Denny family, who built a mansion now known as the Red House. W. \u003Cstrong\u003EGray\u003C\/strong\u003E for twelve years from 1845. As a physician he was clear in diagnosis and skillful in choice of remedies. Lockwood for \u201cInterview with Catherine Harris,\u201d \u003Cem\u003EThe Evening Journal,\u003C\/em\u003E Jamestown, New York, 1902, reprinted in \u003Cem\u003EThe\u003C\/em\u003E \u003Cem\u003EPost Journal\u003C\/em\u003E, Jamestown, New York, February 13, 2011. The father was born in CT about 1779, Harvey, Jr. in NY about 1812. \u00a0One of these men is mentioned in church records in Ripley, NY. The minutes of Session of the Ripley Presbyterian Church in 1836 twice note that a committee looking into Harvey Hall\u2019s \u201ccase\u201d had not seen him to interview him. The entry of 20 November 1837 records that from the committee\u2019s interview with him \u201cthey are somewhat encouraged that he would return to his duty and that no further evidence of his secreting persons escaping from justice has been obtained.\u201d Although it is not specified, his error may have been related to Underground Railroad activity, although other Presbyterian congregations in Chautauqua County at this same time are thought to have been sympathetic to the anti-slavery movement. Since Hall remained in the church, it would seem that either he did not continue as a conductor or stationmaster after 1837, or he rallied other church members to his cause. Harvey Hall and Harvey Hall, Jr. are both found in the 1850 Census but only Harvey Hall, Jr. is listed in the 1855 and later censuses. Comparing the 1855 list with the 1854 map of Chautauqua County and the 1867 county atlas locates Harvey Hall, Jr. in Ripley across from the Methodist Church, which is rumored to have been sympathetic to the anti-slavery underground.\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.5647,42.3077]},"properties":{"name":"Hall","description":"\u003Cp\u003EDavid Hall, the son of Asa and Phoebe (Foster) Hall, was born in Thompson CT on 29 October 1798. Frost (1796 - 1872) settled within what is now known as the village of Cherry Creek in the spring of 1823, and the following compilation of comments about his life are excerpted from Chas. J. Shults, \u003Cem\u003ECherry Creek Illustrated: A History, 1900\u003C\/em\u003E. \u201cFrost lived in the Village until about 1839, when he purchased a large farm situated in a pleasant valley about two miles northwest of the Village on the Fredonia road. His near neighbors were Anson Newton, Harvey Putnam, Ira B. Turner and Elkeny Steward. Mrs. Frost was born in the State of Rhode Island June 25, 1803, and died at Cherry Creek May 27, 1889, surviving him 17 years. Of their marriage twelve children were born.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u0022George H. Frost was a man of strong earnest conviction. Human slavery disturbed the peace and threatened the destruction of the Union. From the time Mr. Frost became old enough to take an interest in public affairs, he was uncompromisingly opposed to the institution of slavery. His entire being revolted at this monster. No subject of public concern affected him more intensely. He believed that this government founded on the equal rights of men could not long survive with slavery. His opposition to it was outspoken, earnest, and active. He early allied himself with the Abolition party, in fact when it required no small degree of moral courage to take a position in the ranks of that despised political sect.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u0022His great opportunities for usefulness to the cause, in his poverty in the wilderness of Western New York, were small compared to those of the leaders of the cause, but he belonged to, and for a long time was actively engaged in the services of the Underground Railroad so-called, and many a fugitive slave was assisted by him on his secret journey in his effort to escape bondage to Canada. French was well known in Dunkirk. His anti-slavery activity was cited in the \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Morning Post\u003C\/em\u003E July 18, 1923 in the reprint of an 1891 story by Palmer K. Shankland. George A. French, son of Asa and Jane French, was born in Massachusetts in August 1798. The family moved to Fredonia by 1810. The Pomfret census for that year lists \u201cA. French\u201d in a family of four: one male 10 to 15, one male 16 to 25, one male 26 to 44, and one female 26 to 44. The other boy is probably the Asa French who died on 1 March 1826, age 25. Young\u2019s \u003Cem\u003EHistory\u003C\/em\u003E (p.471) notes that Asa French was the first blacksmith in Fredonia, with his home and shop on the corner of today\u2019s West Main and Chestnut streets across from Richard Williams\u2019 inn. Jane died on 14 January 1813, age 41, and Asa promptly married Mary Spencer on 28 March of that year.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EWhether it was a motivating factor or not, after Jane died in 1813, Asa gave up the West Hill location and moved to Sheridan. In 1814 he was in the east part of 61-6-11\u00a0 and in 1815 he had 100 acres in the southwest part of 54-6-11. The size of his parcel suggests he was farming, perhaps combining it with blacksmithing. Although their property was in Sheridan, Asa and Mary continued as members of the Fredonia Congregational church, which changed to Presbyterian in 1817, The assessment rolls show Asa French still in Sheridan with some 95 acres in 1819, reduced to 35 acres in 1820 when his son George is listed with the other 35 acres on Lot 54. By 1821 Asa\u2019s name disappears from all records and George has all 70 acres on Lot 54, which continues into 1826. In addition to farming,\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EGeorge must have worked for a merchant during this period since he had enough experience to be able to join with young Walter Smith in opening a store in Sinclairville in 1824. Smith, then just 24, had been apprenticed to Jacob TenEyck in Cazenovia in 1815 and proved so able that TenEyck bankrolled him when he opened a store of his own in Fredonia in 1819, when Smith was all of 19 years old. (Smith had purchased the store and ashery of Joseph and Ralph Plumb, who had gone bankrupt.)\u00a0 In 1825, the Baptist minister Joy Handy joined them as French, Handy \u0026amp; Co. (The \u201c\u0026amp; Co.\u201d was Walter Smith.) That partnership was dissolved in 1828.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EAlthough the Sinclairville store was robbed in 1825, George A. French must have done well enough that on 23 May 1826 he and Walter Smith announced their new partnership in a store in Dunkirk. The assessment roll for 1827 shows George A. French with properties listed as 9 \u0026amp; 10 Front Street (1\/3 acre), 1 Buffalo Street (\u00bc acre), and 24 Front Street (1\/16 acre). It seems to be the 1 Buffalo Street (Washington Avenue) location that was French\u2019s Dunkirk home, noted only as \u201chouse\u201d or \u201cDwelling\u201d in the assessment rolls through 1844. In 1850 it is referred to in parentheses as the Pemberton House.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EGeorge A. French was elected Town Supervisor in 1830 through 1833. On 14 November 1831 French\u2019s wife, Sophia Risley, died and on 25 December 1832 he married Mrs. Sally (Hudson) Day of Dunkirk. She had been a founding member of the Dunkirk Presbyterian Church in 1830. In 1834 he joined. In 1835 he was one of twelve leading citizens who organized the Dunkirk Academy, which opened on 23 September with George A. French as one of the three Trustees. The village of Dunkirk was incorporated in 1837 with Walter Smith as it first president. However, the panic of 1837 devastated the village\u2019s economy, with many businesses forced into foreclosure. It was in May of 1837 that \u201cGeorge A. French \u0026amp; Co.\u201d announced themselves as successors to \u201cJ. Beggs \u0026amp; Co.\u201d which probably included the docks at the foot of Buffalo Street and Center Street.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EIn 1838 French was elected to the NY State Assembly and again in 1840 and 1841. The assessment rolls continue to show him as a prosperous property owner in addition to the important local power he had become. That was his status in September 1851, when he is recorded as receiving a telegraph message from Silas Shearman, an active member of the Underground Railroad in Jamestown, requesting urgent help in rescuing a re-captured refugee slave. The published account of this incident referred to French as \u201ca prominent and zealous Abolitionist.\u201d Shearman knew that \u201cGeorge A. French would arouse the friends of anti-slavery in the vicinity of Dunkirk and \u2026 would give him every possible assistance.\u201d If it were not for this one recorded episode, we would have little record that French had been anything but a popular, successful local businessman.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EFrench\u2019s second wife, Sally, had died on 8 March 1851 some months before the dramatic incidents described. On 8 January 1852 he married Sarah A. Stillman of Wethersfield CT, possibly related to The Rev. Timothy Stillman of the Dunkirk Presbyterian Church. For some reason, French apparently sold his properties. His name disappears from the assessment rolls, although he is still in Dunkirk at the time of the 1860 Census. He died in Fredonia on 29 January 1865 and was buried in Forest Hill Cemetery.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EThe record of George A. Brown was born in Rensselaer County NY in 1794, although the census-taker in 1850 gave his birth place as Pennsylvania. Brown\u2019s wife, Rhoda, was born in Rensselaer County in 1791. He studied to become a lawyer and physician and the two relocated to Busti NY. Since he is mentioned there as \u201cDr. Brown,\u201d he probably worked primarily as a physician. He and his wife can be found in Busti by 1830 running through 1855. He died in Busti on 17 November 1857. John Broadhead was born in Ripponden, West Yorkshire, England to John and Dorothy (Whitworth) Broadhead on 16 January 1798. He joined the Methodist Episcopal Church in 1810, became a class leader in 1814 and a circuit preacher in 1818. On 25 December 1819 he married Hannah Hall. They came to the U.S. in 1827. (John Fletcher Broadhead was born in Hudson NY in 1830. Martha E. Broadhead was born in June 1835 in Chautauqua County NY. ) Hannah Broadhead died on 22 December 1835 and in 1836 Rev. John Broadhead married Elizabeth Cheney of Harmony NY. A memorial account in a Jamestown newspaper, preserved in the \u201cDamon scrap book\u201d describes his strong anti-slavery sentiments. He had been preaching in the Erie Conference \u201cfrom the M. E. Church, which was his choice . . . . but during the years of her slavery agitation he went out and preached for the Wesleyan connection . . . . coming back to his own communion as soon as the Slavery question was settled.\u201d Broadhead\u2019s Underground Railroad activity as a stationmaster was cited in William S. Bailey\u2019s account in \u003Cem\u003EThe Historic Annals of Southwestern New York\u003C\/em\u003E (William Doty, Ed. 1940). There are a number of Broadheads on the 1854 map of Busti including a J. Breed was born in Saratoga NY on 4 April 1804, the son of Thomas Breed. He came to Jamestown NY perhaps as early as 1820 with his older brother, William, who set up as a cabinet maker. In 1826 William took his brother John in as a partner. In the following year, John married Olive, a daughter of Solomon and Clarissa (Hayward) Jones, whose daughter Clara had already been married to William Breed. John Breed worked as a cabinet maker throughout his life. An online posting says he was \u201cthe first of the Baptist faith to settle here and the first superintendent of a Sunday School in the settlement.\u201d This seems to be a partial quotation from some unnamed source. Young\u2019s \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County\u003C\/em\u003E mentions that whatever form the early Baptist gatherings took, they had formed themselves into a conference by April 1832 with John Breed as their Clerk. They reorganized as the Baptist Church of Jamestown in May of that same year. When John C. Breed died in 1886, his obituary in \u003Cem\u003EThe Fredonia Censor\u003C\/em\u003E of 15 December said, \u201cHe was one of the founders of the Baptist church there [Jamestown NY] in 1826.\u201d According to local legend, he and other members of his church were affiliated with the Underground Railroad, and the church history does confirm that he was an ardent abolitionist (Anon., \u003Cem\u003EHistory of the First Baptist Church, Jamestown, NY, 1827 - 1915\u003C\/em\u003E, Journal Press, Jamestown, New York, 1915.)\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.343985908487,41.981555841142]},"properties":{"name":"Blodgett","description":"\u003Cp\u003EArba Blodgett (1789-1837) and Bebe Bullock Blodgett (1790-1878), husband and wife, were active in the Underground Railroad in the vicinity of Busti, NY and Sugar Grove, PA. The following is found in \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County and its People\u003C\/em\u003E, John P. Downs and Fenwick Y. Hedley, Ed., American Historical Society, 1921. \u201cThe Blodgett family left a deep impress upon the history of Busti. The founder, Arba Blodgett, a soldier of the War of 1812, settled in the town near the State line in the southwestern part soon after his military service ended \u2026. He was a strong Abolitionist, and tradition says his home was a station on the Underground Railroad. Loren Blodgett, son of Arba, was known throughout the United States as a statistician, economist and journalist; and his works connected with the Smithsonian Institution and Treasury Department won for him a reputation as one of the world\u0027s greatest statistical compilers\u2026. Arba died in Busti in 1837, meeting an accidental death. Near the Blodgetts lived the family of William Storum, colored, whose daughter married Lewis Clark, a fugitive slave from whose life Harriet Beecher Stowe drew the character of George Harris for \u003Cem\u003EUncle Tom\u0027s Cabin\u003C\/em\u003E. A granddaughter of William Storum married a son of Frederick Douglass. This Storum home was the scene of a cruel incident in 1851, when a runaway slave from the South was taken from there and returned to his old masters.\u201d\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003EArba Blodgett was born in Massachusetts in 1789. In December 1811, he married Reba (Bebe) Bullock, who was born there in 1790. In May 1814 they took a land contract for property in Busti NY, as did William Bullock in October 1814 and Cyrenus Blodgett in June 1815. The two sons, William Oren Blodgett and Lorin Blodgett, seem to have followed their father\u2019s footsteps in the anti-slavery movement. The five daughters, at least some of whom were also active in the anti-slavery cause, were Lucinda Blodgett Langdon (1813-1873), Clarissa M. Blodgett (1816-1887), Adelia Blodgett Smith (1819-1850), Sylvia Blodgett (1827-1849), and Loice (Lois) Blodgett Lott (1829-1915). Bebe later married a widower, Joshua Van Deusen (VanDusen). They were living in the Town of Ellicott at the time of the 1850 Census. Although his name does not appear in Onondaga records, there are many Backus families there from an early date. The earliest record for him is probably the Norman Backus in Salisbury, Herkimer County in the 1830 Census. His wife, Charlotte, was from Herkimer County. By 1840 they had relocated to Clymer NY. Their daughter, Emily, was born in Chautauqua County in 1839. He is not found in the 1850 Census, but by 1855 they were in Busti, where he worked as a cooper. He, his wife, and Emily had been in Busti for 15 years, and their son William had been born there in 1844. The 1860 Census finds him farming in Clymer, but back in Busti as a cooper again in 1865. In 1870 he is listed in Busti as a farm laborer. He and Charlotte probably died soon after that. In 1875 William is found living with his married sister, Caroline, and her husband William Seaver, with no other Backus names in the record. Norman\u2019s anti-slavery activity was cited in the \u003Cem\u003EJamestown Morning Post\u003C\/em\u003E of 18 July 1923 in a reprint of Palmer K. Shankland\u2019s 1891 article from the 18 July 1891 \u003Cem\u003ESaturday Times\u003C\/em\u003E. Although Backus cannot be seen on the 1854 county wall map, several of his neighbors from the 1855 census would place him in or just north of the hamlet of Busti Corners at that time.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}},{"type":"Feature","geometry":{"type":"Point","coordinates":[-79.6297,42.0086]},"properties":{"name":"Amidon","description":"\u003Cp\u003ERev. John Amidon was cited as an Underground Railroad stationmaster by descendants, who contacted\u00a0 the Chautauqua County historian after they found unpublished family records in 2012. John Smith Amidon was born on 2 September 1821 to Leonard and Esther (Smith) Amidon, originally from Connecticut. The family must have removed to NY State fairly early, since John Amidon gave his birth place as Onondaga NY. Young\u2019s \u003Cem\u003EHistory of Chautauqua County\u003C\/em\u003E, p.295, notes that Leonard Amidon contracted for or bought Lot 52 in Twp.1, Range 14, in January 1823. The first local record for Leonard Amidon (sometimes given as Amadon) is in the 1840 Census for Clymer NY. The Methodist Episcopal Church in Clymer organized around 1825. The first class included Lawrence and Adelia Amidon, Elijah and Sarah Amidon, and Leonard and Esther Amidon. When John S. Amidon joined the Methodist Episcopal Church and became a minister is not recorded. He married Charlotte A. Curtis, originally from Madison NY, probably around 1842. Their eldest son, John N., was born in PA in 1845; daughter Apphia in 1851; and William E. in 1854, so John Amidon may have had his early ministry there. Census records for 1855, 1860 and 1870 consistently list him as a farmer. One unverified report, said to be from family records, notes that he operated an Underground Railroad station on the Corry-Clymer Road as well as manning the toll house on what was the Westfield Clymer Plank Road. The toll house can be seen on the 1854 wall map near the L. Amidon farm.\u00a0 Comparing the following homeowners\u2019 family names from the section of the 1855 Census that includes three Amidon households with the names on the 1854 map gives a reasonably accurate location for the home of John S. Amidon that may have been the Underground Railroad station. John S. Amidon died in Corry PA on 3 October 1898.\u003C\/p\u003E\n\u003Cp\u003E\u00a0\u003C\/p\u003E\n"}}]}"
